ðŸ’¬ [1] m3kishiku (6:21:58 PM)
#include <Adafruit_GFX.h>
#include <Adafruit_ILI9341.h>

// Pin setup for Arduino Uno
#define TFT_CS     10
#define TFT_RST    9
#define TFT_DC     8

// Create an instance of the TFT screen
Adafruit_ILI9341 tft = Adafruit_ILI9341(TFT_CS, TFT_DC, TFT_RST);

void setup() {
  // Start the TFT screen
  tft.begin();
  
  // Set rotation (optional, depending on screen orientation)
  tft.setRotation(3); 

  // Fill the screen with a color to test (white in this case)
  tft.fillScreen(ILI9341_WHITE);

  // Set text color and size
  tft.setTextColor(ILI9341_BLACK);
  tft.setTextSize(2);
  
  // Display a message
  tft.setCursor(10, 10);
  tft.println("TFT is working!");
}

void loop() {
  // Nothing to do here for now
}

// For the Adafruit shield, these are the default.
#define TFT_CLK 13
#define TFT_MISO 12
#define TFT_MOSI 11
#define TFT_DC 9
#define TFT_CS 10
#define TFT_RST 8
// Use hardware SPI (on Uno, #13, #12, #11) and the above for CS/DC
//Adafruit_ILI9341 tft = Adafruit_ILI9341(TFT_CS, TFT_DC);
// If using the breakout, change pins as desired
Adafruit_ILI9341 tft = Adafruit_ILI9341(TFT_CS, TFT_DC, TFT_MOSI, TFT_CLK, TFT_RST, TFT_MISO);```

ðŸ’¬ [2] penpengu (8:02:29 AM) [REPLY]
if you know absolutely nothing about arduino then there's a very long but also very good series on youTube "new arduino tutorials" by paul mcworther. 
For now you could think of the arduino mega as just an arduino uno but with more pins, more ram and more flash. So 99% or arduino uno tutorials still apply. (There are some things that the mega can do or can do better than an uno but afaik nothing the other way around)

ðŸ’¬ [3] penpengu (8:02:43 AM)
and bookmark this https://www.arduino.cc/reference/en/

ðŸŽ¯ [4] penpengu (8:03:12 AM)

ðŸ‘‰ the key is to try it as you learn... & keep learning as you go for more & more complicated projects...

ðŸ’¬ [1] .qwertypo (6:17:32 PM)
I think I fixed it

ðŸ’¬ [2] .qwertypo (6:17:55 PM)
I added ` SoftwareSerial rs485(RS485_WASTE, RS485_PIN); // (RX, TX) ` before the setup()

ðŸ’¬ [3] .qwertypo (6:18:18 PM)
And then changed my init to a void() so I could use the same code in the setup and then again in the loop

ðŸŽ¯ [4] .qwertypo (6:18:44 PM)
```
void initializeRS485() {
  bool dipOneState = DIP_ONE_STATE.read() == HIGH;

  // Reconfigure RS485 pins based on DIP switch state
  rs485.end(); // End previous instance
  if (dipOneState) {
    rs485 = SoftwareSerial(RS485_WASTE, RS485_PIN); // TX mode (RX, TX)
    Serial.println("TX Mode");
  } else {
    rs485 = SoftwareSerial(RS485_PIN, RS485_WASTE); // RX mode (RX, TX)
    Serial.println("RX Mode");
  }
  rs485.begin(9600);
}

```

ðŸ’¬ [71] klevi_ (12:42:17 PM) [REPLY]
I think you have the 2 things mixed up.
1: Use analog read to measure a potenetiometers position and use that value to set the speed of a motor or set the position of a servo

2. Use a shunt resistor to measure the current flowing trough it

So the potmeter could be used for setting both the motor speed and servo. Or just one of them, doesn't matter

ðŸ’¬ [107] klevi_ (1:10:14 PM) [REPLY]
So. We would use a voltage drop that is detectable by the UNO but not too high. Using ohms law we can calculate that if we want a 0.2V voltage drop that we can measure. 

Resistance:
I = U / R -> R = U / I 
R = 0.2V / 0.8A = 0.25ohms.

Power rating
P = U * I
P = 0.2 * 0.8 = 0.16W

ðŸ’¬ [108] dapilot954 (1:12:30 PM) [REPLY]
Elegoo EL-KIT-001 UNO R3 Project Complete Starter Kit with Tutorial for Arduino (63 Items) https://amzn.eu/d/7PwJlHW

ðŸ’¬ [109] dapilot954 (1:12:54 PM) [REPLY]
Alright

ðŸ’¬ [110] klevi_ (1:13:30 PM) [REPLY]
At a 0.2V voltage drop using the UNO (5 / 1028 = 0.0048V steps of the ADC) so the voltage drop divided by the steps ~5mV -> 0.2/0.0048 = 41.66 steps.
800mA/41.66 = 19mA per step.

ðŸ’¬ [111] klevi_ (1:14:14 PM)
You can adjust the values and calculate it yourself to get the resolution you need

ðŸ’¬ [112] dellungi (1:14:51 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸ’¬ [161] penpengu (1:42:52 PM) [REPLY]
SD-cards themselves run on 3.3V.
If you have an Arduino Uno, you need an SD-card reader module that has level shifting and 5V->3.3V regulator.
If you have an ESP32 or other similar MCU with 3.3V logic level you need an SD-card reader that has no level shifting.

ðŸ’¬ [184] penpengu (1:53:09 PM) [REPLY]
that's one with level shifting and 5V->3.3V regulator made for unos. 
If I understand it correctly, then it should still work with an ESP32. Yes, it needs 5V in this case.
But you could switch to a way smaller SD card reader.

ðŸ’¬ [185] penpengu (1:53:18 PM)
smaller micro sd breakout without the unnecessary level shifter and voltage regulator.

ðŸ’¬ [186] ixxcs (1:53:38 PM)
unavailable here

ðŸ’¬ [187] ixxcs (1:54:03 PM) [REPLY]
so why wouldn't it work on 3.3V with esp32?

ðŸ’¬ [188] penpengu (1:56:15 PM) [REPLY]
because of the voltage regulator
The "for 5V MCUs" SD-card readers have a voltage regulator that regulates 5V down to 3.3V for the sd-card. If you supply that voltage regulator with less than 4.8V then I don't know what it outputs. Probably not 3.3V so the Sd-card doesn't work.
Because you have an ESP32 you can use a breakout board that doesn't have the useless voltage regulator and supply the sd-card directly with 3.3V

ðŸ’¬ [189] ixxcs (1:57:24 PM) [REPLY]
i see so it isn't working on 3.3V because its made for arduino's 5V and expects a 5V even tho it could run on 3.3V but the regulator doesn't let it.

ðŸ’¬ [190] penpengu (1:57:41 PM)
yes

ðŸ’¬ [59] anonengineering (12:50:28 PM)
an Uno, that controls a servo, and has an LCD display & buzzer

ðŸ’¬ [60] ecz_na1 (12:50:46 PM) [REPLY]
yes

ðŸ’¬ [61] ecz_na1 (12:50:57 PM)
heres my idea right

ðŸ’¬ [62] ecz_na1 (12:50:58 PM)
Interactive potentiometer game. The tilt switch controls the status of the entire Arduino, it turns on or off the game. The potentiometer decides the servo's position, and the push button is used to score a point and turn off an LED. The game will function as one of 4 LEDs will light up randomly and we need to position the servo using the potentiometer to point towards the LED that is lit up. When it is positioned correctly we will press the push button, it will play a sound and will display 1 representing a point on the LCD. The score will increment by one every time you score a successful point. If the button is pushed when the servo isn't pointing toward the lit-up LED, Incorrect will be displayed on the same LCD, and the score will reset as a sign of losing the game.

ðŸ’¬ [2] fakemayham_0.8.4_ (2:03:17 PM)
1. Check the Port and Board Selection:
Ensure that the correct board is selected in the Arduino IDE:
Go to Tools > Board > Arduino Uno.
Verify that the correct port is selected:
Go to Tools > Port and select the port associated with your Arduino Uno.

2. Check USB Connection:
Ensure the USB cable is properly connected and not damaged.
Try using a different USB port on your computer or a different cable.

3. Restart the Arduino IDE:
Close and reopen the Arduino IDE to ensure that there are no lingering issues.

4. Reboot Your Computer:
Restart your computer to clear any potential conflicts or issues with the USB driver.

5. Disable Other Programs:
Close other programs that might be using the serial port (e.g., other serial monitor applications).

6. Reset the Arduino Board:
Press the reset button on the Arduino Uno before uploading the sketch.

7. Reinstall USB Drivers:
Reinstall the Arduino USB drivers:
Go to Device Manager (Windows), find the Arduino under Ports (COM & LPT), right-click, and select Update Driver or reinstall the driver from the Arduino IDE installation folder.

8. Check Sketch for Issues:
Ensure that your sketch does not have errors. You can enable verbose output in File > Preferences to get more detailed information on the error.

9. Test with a Simple Sketch:
Upload a simple sketch (like the Blink example) to see if the issue is with your current sketch:
Go to File > Examples > 01.Basics > Blink.

10. Reinstall Arduino IDE:
If the issue persists, try reinstalling the Arduino IDE.

ðŸ’¬ [7] toph2814 (7:38:52 AM) [REPLY]
if you're using Phil's Pico board core then you just set the pins with `SPI.setSCK( usedClockPin );` then simply call `SPI.begin( true );` *(after setting all of your pins to enable hardware SPI; use `SPI1` if you want to set the second channel)*

ðŸ’¬ [8] toph2814 (7:39:39 AM)

ah or another thing. ESP32 serial prints some debug information when it starts. That debug information is at a different baud rate so it looks garbled. But you don't care about it. So if there's a bunch of garbage when the ESP32 starts and everything that you Serial.print works, then you can just ignore the garbage.

ðŸ’¬ [163] toph2814 (9:22:15 PM) [REPLY]
monochromatic LCDs are fairly rare in the hobby community *(outside of the 16x2 & 20x4 displays powered by Hitachi HD44780 or compatible display driver ICs)*
ðŸ¤” the TWW240128A9-A0 display you linked has a 240x128 resolution... have you considered using an e-Paper display instead?
ðŸ‘‡ they are a bit more expensive than LCDs but have major advantages when it comes to display readability in ambient light & power consumption *(if that is a concern)*...  this 2.13" *Waveshare*  module *(similar products are commonly used as remotely updatable price tags at large chain supermarkets)*  has a resolution of 250X122 ... *(2.9" 296x128 modules are also common)* ...

ðŸ’¬ [164] toph2814 (9:24:06 PM)
*(btw, these Waveshare ones have a decent amount of documentation & driver support but you need to dig through them & tinker with them since they are not very well organized)*

ðŸ’¬ [165] toph2814 (9:25:08 PM)
yes... that's the drawback of e-Paper/e-Ink displays but there are ways around it with partial updates *(if you don't mind the flickering involved in making the display look crisp)*

ðŸ’¬ [166] toph2814 (9:25:56 PM)
â˜ï¸ ghosting occurs if you don't flush the screen *(the necessary flickering when you flip any aassigned black pixel to white & white pixel to black before changing it to the color you want)*

ðŸ’¬ [167] toph2814 (9:28:44 PM)
ðŸ¤” it's an opinion.... I've seen them starting to appear on e-Bikes & it looks very clean & much more premium than monochrome LCD screens imo...

ðŸ’¬ [168] toph2814 (9:29:43 PM)
ðŸ‘‰ the thing is, since you are making something bespoke & for commercial consumption, I would recommend looking at manufacturers of said displays... so not for the hobby market...

ðŸ’¬ [169] toph2814 (9:30:14 PM)
ðŸ¤” I imagine they may even have models that have better refresh rates *(they are getting better if you look at the eBook market)*

ðŸ’¬ [170] toph2814 (9:31:05 PM)
shoot... perhaps someone can help...

ðŸ’¬ [171] toph2814 (9:31:56 PM)
*(btw, support: documentation, driver software, etc ~is the reason why I recommend connecting with manufacturers directly since they will provide all of this to you when you order in bulk)*

ðŸŽ¯ [1] anonengineering (3:44:00 PM)
a "4-20mA" current loop is a fairly common industrial control system method of sending data.
with an additional module like shown here <https://www.instructables.com/Interfacing-4-20mA-Current-Loop-Sensors-With-Ardui/> you can "talk" to the sensor using I2C

ðŸ’¬ [2] anonengineering (3:47:55 PM)
well, the actual resolution you get depends on the sensor... if you are looking for 0.001 psi you may or may not get it ðŸ˜‰

ðŸ’¬ [3] anonengineering (3:49:27 PM)
you can "hang" up to 127 I2C devices on the bus (as long as they have unique addresses)

ðŸ’¬ [4] anonengineering (3:50:23 PM)


ðŸ’¬ [5] anonengineering (3:51:39 PM)
I2C is meant for short wires (4-20mA allows for long wire runs)

ðŸ’¬ [6] anonengineering (3:52:18 PM)
yes

ðŸ’¬ [7] anonengineering (3:54:44 PM)
correct, each device has a Clock (SCL) and a data line (SDA)
only one set of pull up resistors is needed, they might be on the modules already

ðŸ’¬ [8] anonengineering (3:55:48 PM)
SCL and SDA are referred to as a "bus", a set of parallel wires

ðŸ’¬ [9] anonengineering (3:56:33 PM)
good, then you aren't picturing a big vehicle to move ppl ðŸ™‚

ðŸ’¬ [10] anonengineering (3:57:20 PM)
similar ig to a 70v speaker system

ðŸ’¬ [11] anonengineering (3:58:27 PM)
we each have our specialties, haven't played with audio in years...

ðŸ’¬ [12] anonengineering (4:02:58 PM)
yes, was just illustrating that the real question you need to answer isn't directly related to that sensor but rather "how do I connect an Uno to a 4-20mA current loop"

ðŸ’¬ [13] anonengineering (4:04:07 PM)
bear in mind the sensor wants 8v, an Uno can handle max 5v

PWM Signal Frequency: 50 Hz
â€‹
Throttle Control:
1 ms pulse width: Zero throttle
2 ms pulse width: Full throttle

The PWM signal operates on a spectrum between these endpoints, allowing for incremental control of the throttle between 1 ms (zero throttle) and 2 ms (full throttle).

ðŸ’¬ [98] cravenchicken_right_now (10:58:08 PM)
PWM Frequency: 8kHz, 16kHz
 
     19. 8kHz: Output Frequency to the motor. Generally used for inrunner motors with lower pole counts, more power-efficient, causes less Electromagnetic Interference (EMI), and is the ESC's default setting.

     20. 16kHz: Output Frequency to the motor. PWM frequency is generally used for outrunner motors because the motor will run smoother and with slightly more power but at the cost of the ESC running hotter causing more Electromagnetic Interference (EMI).

ðŸ’¬ [79] anonengineering (11:16:04 PM)
```c++
// the setup function runs once when you press reset or power the board
void setup() {
  // initialize digital pin LED_BUILTIN as an output.
  pinMode(LED_BUILTIN, OUTPUT);
}

// the loop function runs over and over again forever
void loop() {
  digitalWrite(LED_BUILTIN, HIGH);  // turn the LED on (HIGH is the voltage level)
  delay(1000);                      // wait for a second
  digitalWrite(LED_BUILTIN, LOW);   // turn the LED off by making the voltage LOW
  delay(1000);                      // wait for a second
}```

Normal uploading over USB basically works like this:
- Reset the microcontroller
- Bootloader starts
- Hey Bootloader! I want to upload a new program, here is it:
- Bootloader programs the program onto the microcontroller.
- bootloader starts your code

ðŸ’¬ [34] robherc (12:15:20 PM) [REPLY]
Yes, you *can* burn a "barebones" firmware to your 328P over ICSP that does not include any form of arduino bootloader. ... HOWEVER, as per #code-of-conduct we are not allowed to recommend that you do this, and even without that rule, It is very highly recommended against doing that unless you are ***fully certain*** that it's exactly what you want to do, and you're aware of why.

ðŸ’¬ [35] robherc (12:17:08 PM) [REPLY]
Meanwhile, the good news is that if you learn on your own how to ICSP-flash your custom bootloader-free sketch, you ***should*** learn enough in the process to be able to readily re-flash the arduino bootloader back onto your board using the "Burn Bootloader" option in the IDE.

```#include <ArduinoOTA.h>
#include <WiFi.h>

const char* ssid = "your-SSID";
const char* password = "your-PASSWORD";

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(9600);
  WiFi.begin(ssid, password);
  
  while (WiFi.status() != WL_CONNECTED) {
    delay(500);
    Serial.print(".");
  }
  Serial.println("\nWiFi Connected");

  ArduinoOTA.begin();
}

void loop() {
  ArduinoOTA.handle();
}``` i used this and just edited it and uploaded it via WiFi

ðŸ’¬ [8] van059349 (8:18:30 AM)
Is there a way to change a default pins set of a module? Lets say i download a library for a certain module, and it tells me to connect to certain pins to arduino (d5, d6 or whatever). Is there a way to change that? Because i dont see it in the code. Is there a hidden part of the code where those pins are set?

ðŸ’¬ [9] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (8:22:49 AM) [REPLY]
It depends. For hardware i2c on the Uno R3 for example, you cannot change which pins are used.

ðŸ’¬ [10] van059349 (8:23:38 AM) [REPLY]
What is uno r3?

ðŸ’¬ [11] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (8:25:19 AM) [REPLY]
An Arduino board.

ðŸ’¬ [12] van059349 (8:26:06 AM) [REPLY]
Why does it care wgat pins u use? Arent all d pins the same?

ðŸ’¬ [13] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (8:28:50 AM) [REPLY]
No, in this specific example, the hardware i2c and spi interfaces on the Atmega328 are only connected to specific pins.
Many other things can use any pins, just these things in particular cannot.
There are microcontrollers that allow you to use any pins you want with the hardware i2c and spi interfaces, the Atmega328 is just not one of them.
You haven't mentioned any of the hardware or libraries you're using, i'm just explaining a situation in which you would not be able to change the pins.

ðŸ’¬ [15] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (10:37:57 AM) [REPLY]
COM1 is not the correct port. Does the board appear in windows device manager at any point in the process?

ðŸ’¬ [16] mycydric7 (10:38:57 AM) [REPLY]
used to be, but now it just shows COM1

ðŸ’¬ [17] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (10:39:58 AM) [REPLY]
After placing a jumper between the GND and download pins on the 6 pin header, the board should appear as some type of ESP32-S3.

ðŸŽ¯ [19] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (8:26:41 AM) [REPLY]
The bootloader prints a message at 74880 baud when the board reboots.
So, the serial monitor would need to be at 74880 baud to read it properly.
You should be able to tell if the chip is in download mode based on the message.

ðŸŽ¯ [7] klevi_ (1:28:15 PM) [REPLY]
Or just the load is great enough to trigger some protection circuits either in the Arduino or the laptop.  This can damage both of them
- What is the power consumption of your bigger servo? And how much current can USB2.0 and the UNO provide? You will find the problem there 99% sure

ðŸ’¬ [8] makiz.zz (3:46:20 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸ’¬ [9] makiz.zz (3:48:41 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸ’¬ [10] klevi_ (3:50:49 PM) [REPLY]
So 500-900mA for normal operation means that on startup it could be several times that amount. That will damage your Arduino and your laptop/PC.

Batteries are a great idea. Take note of their current rating as well

ðŸ’¬ [11] makiz.zz (4:46:18 PM) [REPLY]
laptops have safety features for that, however its weird, it should be able to work

ðŸ’¬ [12] robherc (4:51:32 PM) [REPLY]
wow, wouldn't it be lovely if that were a true statement! (sorry, most don't have 1/4 of the safety features they should)

ðŸ’¬ [13] klevi_ (4:51:32 PM) [REPLY]
Either that safety system or the Arduino's safety system (poly fuse) made the Arduino reset/lose connection.
- Just because your household electrical circuit has fuses, you don't go poking it with forks, shorting random stuff and electricuting yourself.

ðŸ’¬ [30] anonengineering (10:25:28 PM)
```c++
//TMP36 Pin Variables
int sensorPin = A0; //the analog pin the TMP36's Vout (sense) pin is connected to
                        //the resolution is 10 mV / degree centigrade with a
                        //500 mV offset to allow for negative temperatures
 
/*
 * setup() - this function runs once when you turn your Arduino on
 * We initialize the serial connection with the computer
 */
void setup()
{
  Serial.begin(9600);  //Start the serial connection with the computer
                       //to view the result open the serial monitor 
}
 
void loop()                     // run over and over again
{
 //getting the voltage reading from the temperature sensor
 int reading = analogRead(sensorPin);  
 
 // converting that reading to voltage, for 3.3v arduino use 3.3
 float voltage = reading * 5.0;
 voltage /= 1024.0; 
 
 // print out the voltage
 Serial.print(voltage); Serial.println(" volts");
 
 // now print out the temperature
 float temperatureC = (voltage - 0.5) * 100 ;  //converting from 10 mv per degree wit 500 mV offset
                                               //to degrees ((voltage - 500mV) times 100)
 Serial.print(temperatureC); Serial.println(" degrees C");
 
 // now convert to Fahrenheit
 float temperatureF = (temperatureC * 9.0 / 5.0) + 32.0;
 Serial.print(temperatureF); Serial.println(" degrees F");
 
 delay(1000);                                     //waiting a second
}```
run that, it's a "known good" example

ðŸ’¬ [31] anonengineering (10:26:31 PM)
assuming the sensor is hooked to A0

ðŸ’¬ [32] anonengineering (10:27:04 PM)
<https://learn.adafruit.com/tmp36-temperature-sensor/using-a-temp-sensor>

ðŸ’¬ [33] anonengineering (10:28:42 PM)
no, an Uno is a 5v board, those comments are if you are using a 3.3v board

ðŸ’¬ [1] sofree._ (3:47:44 PM)
Hey I have a project to make a gait analysis device using arduino and mpu6050 sensors. I need to measure knee angle and also foot angle and foot strike and to validate the device against camera tracking software (SIMI camera) Iâ€™m having issues with I2C connections where it would work for a moment and then it would completely stop working for a long time. I tried adding a whoami loop to the code but it only ran once and I could not test.

ðŸ’¬ [2] vilksian (4:11:37 PM) [REPLY]
You're essentially making body sensors, so are the I2C devices a couple feet away from the controller? If so, I know I2C has limits on how far the signal can go on a bus before it becomes unreliable, and is affected by stray capacitance. IIRC faster data transfer is affected by this capacitance more, so in theory, slower I2C communication should be more reliable with these longer distances.

ðŸ’¬ [3] sofree._ (4:55:38 PM) [REPLY]
Whatttttt so if I use slave master connections would that help? I didnâ€™t know about this at all and also if thereâ€™s any sources or material I can read to help me with this? Appreciate your help

ðŸ’¬ [4] sofree._ (4:56:40 PM) [REPLY]
They are roughly 40cm away from the sensor placed on the knee and around 80 to the second sensor placed on the shank

ðŸ’¬ [5] vilksian (5:14:59 PM) [REPLY]
I'm not great at explaining how capacitance affects the signal unfortunately (despite knowing what the *result* can look like), but one thing I can suggest is maybe trying to change the I2C clock speed if the libraries your using allow it. See about slowing it down. 
[This stack exchange post](https://electronics.stackexchange.com/questions/106265/maximum-i2c-bus-length) might help you understand it a bit.

ðŸ’¬ [6] vilksian (5:17:23 PM)
I've actually been trying to solve this problem myself for one of my own projects, and haven't figured out a "pretty" solution yet.

ðŸ’¬ [7] sofree._ (5:24:29 PM) [REPLY]
I have 2 weeks to figure it out itâ€™s for my final year project Iâ€™m so cooked

ðŸ’¬ [8] _wee_gee_ (5:24:43 PM) [REPLY]
I2C uses pull-up resistors to bring the signal lines "back up" to power, and each device on the line can choose to pull the line "low"

If you have stray capacitances, then this can slow down how fast the pull resistors can bring the line back up to a High logic level, which hard-limits your maximum clock rate.

The distance of the pull resistors to the sensor also dictates this. 
Some pre-made modules have the resistors built in, and can sometimes end up with too many pull resistors making the High "too strong" to pull down for each sensor.

You get an R-C time constant when the line is going from low to high, where R is the pull resistors and C is the stray capacitance.
Here's a link to [Hyperphysics](http://hyperphysics.phy-astr.gsu.edu/hbase/electric/capchg.html) talking about RC time constants and some of the analog world you're going to need to worry about when running wires that long and using I2C

ðŸ’¬ [9] _wee_gee_ (5:25:30 PM)
If this is a final year project then you should (hopefully) have been exposed to Circuits classes which talk about these things, so time to apply that knowledge of impulse response of a circuit!

ðŸŽ¯ [10] _wee_gee_ (5:25:48 PM)
(or, you can just slow the I2C down until it works 'goodenuf')

ðŸ’¬ [11] sofree._ (5:26:46 PM)
I have a previous years code that did this project. Iâ€™m expanding on it by adding foot strike and foot angle measurements. Idk how they were able to get results I think they may have lied

ðŸ’¬ [12] _wee_gee_ (5:28:23 PM) [REPLY]
To add to this, you should also make sure that the sensor modules that you're using have a proper "tank" or "decoupling" capacitor to be able to pull the I2C lines low when needed, else they need to "wait" for power to travel down the entire line, slowing edges even worse (or in the worst case, completely starving the sensor of power)

ðŸ’¬ [13] _wee_gee_ (5:28:51 PM) [REPLY]
That's a bold claim to make when you're adding hardware that's so far away and seemingly only now seeing problems

ðŸ’¬ [14] _wee_gee_ (5:29:35 PM) [REPLY]
Especially when there's reasonable explanations such as this for why you're having problems

ðŸ’¬ [15] sofree._ (5:29:46 PM) [REPLY]
My lecturer said they had similar problems and it was a mess up until the end and another person is getting measurements for arm elbow joint and yea heâ€™s using gpt and his results donâ€™t seem to make any sense

ðŸ’¬ [16] sofree._ (5:30:40 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸ’¬ [17] _wee_gee_ (5:32:18 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸ’¬ [1] nikkis_blah (10:58:42 AM) [REPLY]
ok,i meant that
on starting the drone up,a resistor connects the arduino to the battery,to make it start at 5 volts
then the arduino starts a pwm signal on one of its 6 pwm pins,that is used as the gate in the dc "step-down"/buck converter,which supplies the arduino with 5 volts from the battery with hopefully less energy losses than the resistor

ðŸ’¬ [2] nikkis_blah (10:58:57 AM)
then the resistor is disconnected

ðŸ’¬ [3] nikkis_blah (10:59:30 AM)
and by that i mean a relay switches it off from a signal from the arduino

ðŸ’¬ [4] robherc (11:25:50 AM) [REPLY]
I would use a 5v LDO-regulator instead of a "resistor" here, and a MOSFET transistor instead of a relay, but your basic premise seems workable.

ðŸ’¬ [5] nikkis_blah (11:34:27 AM) [REPLY]
how would the mosfet be open for the resistor to supply current?it needs voltage on its gate right?

ðŸ’¬ [6] nikkis_blah (11:37:16 AM)
actually,ill just use a push button to turn it on,and release the button when an led that signals the pwm signal is running
since a relay will need constant supply to keep closed if i route the resistor through the "always on" path in the relay

ðŸŽ¯ [7] robherc (11:43:50 AM) [REPLY]
JFETs and some MOSFETs work in "depletion mode" where their default state is "on" ... and pull-up/pull-down resistors to give a regular MOSFET a "default on" state are easy to use as well. ... then the arduino just pulls against the resistor to "switch the MOSFET OFF" once it has the buck converter running stably.

ðŸ’¬ [127] toph2814 (7:24:48 AM)
it all depends on your motors @Deleted User ... the L298N will drop 2V so if you feed it a 3S lithium power source, you will be giving the motor attached to it anywhere from 6V upto as high as 10.6V depending on the battery cells' state of charge

ðŸ’¬ [128] toph2814 (7:25:22 AM)
*(so it will be fine if your motors are rated at 6V-12V DC... but if it's only 3V-6V, you will burn your motors out sooner than later)*

ðŸ’¬ [129] Deleted User (7:32:19 AM) [REPLY]
heres what the seller said

ðŸ’¬ [130] Deleted User (7:32:31 AM)
so i reckon i should probably lower it to 2s then

ðŸ’¬ [131] Deleted User (7:32:40 AM)
what do you think? or is lowering it not necessary

ðŸ’¬ [132] toph2814 (7:45:33 AM) [REPLY]
although motors can work above their rated voltages doing so will burn out their coils sooner than later, shortening its operational life...
ðŸ‘‰ so using a 2S lithium battery pack provides from 5.5V to 8.4V, the motor driver's drop will leave you with around 3.5V to 6.4V for your motors which would work fine *(since lithium cells don't stay at their peak/full voltages for very long)*.

ðŸ’¬ [133] toph2814 (7:48:06 AM)
ðŸ¤“ naturally, if you want more consistent performance from your motors *(rather than dependent on the battery's state of charge)* ... you could always use your 3S or 2S pack & use a buck boost converter to keep it at a constant voltage *(like 5.5V so you're not stressing your motors too much)*

ðŸ’¬ [134] toph2814 (7:50:36 AM)
âš ï¸ naturally, your converter should be able to handle your expected load & then some as a safety margin... so 4 motors x 350mA each(max) x 20% safety margin == 1.68A *(or a `2A buck boost converter`)*

ðŸ’¬ [135] toph2814 (7:53:25 AM)
it's due to the L298N driver's 2V voltage drop @Deleted User so they'll need to feed it more voltage so that more voltage reaches the motors or use a different motor driver

ðŸ’¬ [136] toph2814 (7:55:16 AM)
ðŸ¤“ for more information about what's available for hobbyists & tinkerers, check out Bill's article *(& video)*  on the subject...
ðŸ“š https://dronebotworkshop.com/dc-motor-drivers/

ðŸ’¬ [137] Deleted User (8:00:55 AM) [REPLY]

What kind of board is it? You might have bricked it and will have to program it with an ICSP programmer.
ATtiny isn't really beginner friendly. The bootloader is not write protected. It also takes up 25% of the available flash memory for program storage. It's really better to just use a programmer.

ðŸ’¬ [8] toph2814 (1:51:01 PM) [REPLY]
the IR proximity sensor module is designed to use the IR transmitter to send a pulse that goes off ahead of it until it is reflected by an object that is close enough back to the IR receiver....
ðŸ‘‰ the receiver's reading is passed to a comparator that signals `HIGH` when it detects a reflected pulse based on the sensitivity set by the onboard potentiometer *(the blue box)*...

ðŸ’¬ [9] toph2814 (1:51:50 PM)
ðŸ˜… so short answer is "no".... *(at least not without major surgery on the module)*

```c++
void testSegments() {
    int segs[] = {a, b, c, d, e, f, g};
    for (int i = 0; i < 7; i++) {
        digitalWrite(segs[i], HIGH);
        delay(500);
        digitalWrite(segs[i], LOW);
    }
}```

void setup() {
 
  // Start Serial Monitor
  Serial.begin(115200);
 
  // Setup WiFi in Station mode
  WiFi.disconnect();
  WiFi.mode(WIFI_STA);
  WiFi.begin(ssid.c_str(), password.c_str());
 
  while (WiFi.status() != WL_CONNECTED) {
    delay(500);
    Serial.print(".");
  }
 
  // WiFi Connected, print IP to serial monitor
  Serial.println("");
  Serial.println("WiFi connected");
  Serial.println("IP address: ");
  Serial.println(WiFi.localIP());
  Serial.println("");

ðŸ’¬ [4] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (9:32:02 PM) [REPLY]
No code should cause the ESP32 to overheat.

ðŸ’¬ [5] arvincible (9:45:40 PM) [REPLY]
Not kidding mate, I thought it was because I was using Wifi so i uploaded another code which uses Wifi and esp32 cooled down using that code. Troubleshooting led me to conclude the code I sent is overheating my esp32

ðŸ’¬ [6] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (9:47:39 PM) [REPLY]
The voltage regulator was probably damaged and can no longer provide the current needed for WiFi without issues. It's not the code, it's the current draw.

ðŸ’¬ [7] arvincible (9:48:56 PM) [REPLY]
but in both tests i did the esp32 has nothing connected to the pins, it is just running while connected to my computer via usb

ðŸŽ¯ [8] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (9:50:13 PM) [REPLY]
The ESP32 microcontroller on the dev board is drawing current from the voltage regulator on the board that supplies it's power. It draws a lot of current when using WiFi.
This regulator is also connected to the 3.3v pin which you previously had connected to the audio amplifier which was probably drawing enough current in combination with the ESP32 to damage the regulator or something else on the board.

ðŸ’¬ [9] arvincible (9:53:48 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸŽ¯ [1] .pseud0 (3:18:42 PM)
> my problem is that the arduino is getting power witouth connecting the barrel jack
I have seen multiple cases of parasitic powering due to a voltage being present on the microcontroller's I/O pins, which in turn have a I/O protection diode connected between them and e.g. 5V. And hence, voltage can leak in to the voltage net of the microcontroller's supply voltage.

ðŸ’¬ [2] .pseud0 (3:19:05 PM) [REPLY]
Specifically as seen in <https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=2yFh7Vv0Paw>

ðŸ’¬ [19] _wee_gee_ (4:31:26 AM)
```c
long readVcc() { 
  long result; // Read 1.1V reference against AVcc 
  ADMUX = _BV(REFS0) | _BV(MUX3) | _BV(MUX2) | _BV(MUX1);
  delay(2); // Wait for Vref to settle 
  ADCSRA |= _BV(ADSC); // Convert 
  while(bit_is_set(ADCSRA,ADSC));
  result = ADCL;
  result |= ADCH<<8;
  result = 1126400L / result; // Back-calculate AVcc in mV
  return result;
}
void setup() { Serial.begin(9600); }

void loop() {
  Serial.println( readVcc(), DEC );
  delay(1000);
} 
```
This is from wayback's archive of a project on *google code*
<https://web.archive.org/web/20240719170912/https://code.google.com/archive/p/tinkerit/wikis/SecretVoltmeter.wiki>

ðŸ’¬ [20] _wee_gee_ (4:31:44 AM)
Looks like it's still there on the actual link though
<https://code.google.com/archive/p/tinkerit/wikis/SecretVoltmeter.wiki>

ðŸ’¬ [1] dragonkingsw (11:02:29 AM)
i need help with my NRF24L01 moduels and getting them to work. The transmitter send Hello World To The Serial Monitor and should sen it to the other arduino but there nothing appears. here is the code for the transmitter and reciver:
Transmitter:
/*
* Arduino Wireless Communication Tutorial
*     Example 1 - Transmitter Code
*                
* by Dejan Nedelkovski, www.HowToMechatronics.com
* 
* Library: TMRh20/RF24, https://github.com/tmrh20/RF24/
*/

#include <SPI.h>
#include <nRF24L01.h>
#include <RF24.h>

RF24 radio(7, 8); // CE, CSN

const byte address[6] = "00001";

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(9600);
  radio.begin();
  radio.openWritingPipe(address);
  radio.setPALevel(RF24_PA_MIN);
  radio.stopListening();
}

void loop() {
  const char text[32] = "Hello World";
  radio.write(&text, sizeof(text));
  Serial.println(text);
  delay(1000);
}

Receiver:
/*
* Arduino Wireless Communication Tutorial
*       Example 1 - Receiver Code
*                
* by Dejan Nedelkovski, www.HowToMechatronics.com
* 
* Library: TMRh20/RF24, https://github.com/tmrh20/RF24/
*/

#include <SPI.h>
#include <nRF24L01.h>
#include <RF24.h>

RF24 radio(7, 8); // CE, CSN

const byte address[6] = "00001";

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(9600);
  radio.begin();
  radio.openReadingPipe(0, address);
  radio.setPALevel(RF24_PA_MIN);
  radio.startListening();
}

void loop() {
  if (radio.available()) {
    char text[32] = "";
    radio.read(&text, sizeof(text));
    Serial.println(text);
  }
}

ðŸŽ¯ [2] dragonkingsw (11:03:14 AM)
i dont know if its hardware or code problem

ðŸ’¬ [11] .pseud0 (7:46:31 AM) [REPLY]
why, an Arduino only has like 5V or 3.3V of output voltage capability?

ðŸ’¬ [12] .pseud0 (7:46:47 AM)
it's not about the input power supply range, it's about the drive capability of the chip

ðŸŽ¯ [13] .pseud0 (7:47:48 AM)
e.g., this chip can **sink** 16mA max, but only source 0.4mA.

ðŸ’¬ [14] .pseud0 (7:48:31 AM)
so, an inverted LED setup where, the output of the logic gate goes to the negative side of the LED, with the other side of the LED being connected to e.g. +5V throug a 1 kohm resistor, works better, because it can sink currrent better than it can source

ðŸ’¬ [19] anonengineering (9:15:37 AM) [REPLY]
go to #bot-commands and type !pullup
that description talks about Arduino but it applies to logic gates as well
you can't leave a gate input "floating", it will act like an antenna, which is what I see in your video

ðŸ’¬ [20] tristan_123456 (9:26:39 AM) [REPLY]
Il try ty

ðŸ’¬ [21] anonengineering (9:27:47 AM) [REPLY]
<https://wokwi.com/projects/428668737095576577>

ðŸ’¬ [22] .pseud0 (9:29:28 AM) [REPLY]
if your buttons connect the input of the logic gate to +5V when pressed, then you need a pull-down resistor on the input gate to prevent the floating (undefined) input voltage when the button is **not** pressed

ðŸ’¬ [23] .pseud0 (9:29:37 AM)
e.g., 10 kOhm between each logic gate input and GND.

THREAD #107

Messages: 3 | Duration: 2 minutes

ðŸ’¬ [1] toph2814 (11:17:57 AM)
@Chris SaltPrairiePhotography, remember that Arduino boards are meant to control components & read from sensors... they are __not meant__ to supply any significant amount of power since they are not designed for that.
ðŸ‘‰ pulling more current from something like the 3.3V pin will potentially strain the Every's internal regulator... your best reference is the official schematic diagram:
ðŸ“š https://content.arduino.cc/assets/NANOEveryV3.0_sch.pdf

ðŸŽ¯ [2] toph2814 (11:19:10 AM)
which tells you that it uses the AP2112-3.3 regulator IC...
ðŸ“š https://www.diodes.com/assets/Datasheets/AP2112.pdf

ðŸ’¬ [3] toph2814 (11:19:32 AM)
*(which in turn tells you how much current you can safely pull off it)*

ðŸŽ¯ [1] ja_pinkerton (1:50:35 AM)
sectors are an artifact of when we stored data on spinning rust

ðŸ’¬ [2] ja_pinkerton (1:52:37 AM)
calder - have you read the docs?

ðŸ’¬ [3] ja_pinkerton (1:54:57 AM)
because it looks kinda easy.    But I suspect your question is "how do I know what to write where?   How do I know where to read what I wrote?"

ðŸ’¬ [4] ja_pinkerton (1:56:30 AM)
And I think the answer is "flash memory isn't a file system."     You want to allocate the addresses by hand or at least at compile time.  So you know that address 0 is always one thing, and address 128 is some other thing, and address 132 is a third thing

ðŸ’¬ [83] flamingo3438 (2:28:47 PM)
#include <SoftwareSerial.h>
#include <AFMotor.h>

// Bluetooth module setup
SoftwareSerial BTSerial(10, 11); // RX (Arduino 10) <-> TX (HC-05), TX (Arduino 11) <-> RX (HC-05)

// Motor shield setup
AF_DCMotor motor1(1); // Front-left motor
AF_DCMotor motor2(2); // Front-right motor
AF_DCMotor motor3(3); // Rear-left motor
AF_DCMotor motor4(4); // Rear-right motor

// Speed configuration (0-255)
const int motorSpeed = 150;

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(9600);   // Debug serial
  BTSerial.begin(9600); // HC-05 default baud rate
  
  // Set motor speeds
  motor1.setSpeed(motorSpeed);
  motor2.setSpeed(motorSpeed);
  motor3.setSpeed(motorSpeed);
  motor4.setSpeed(motorSpeed);
  
  Serial.println("Voice-Controlled Wheelchair Ready");
  Serial.println("Waiting for Bluetooth commands...");
}

ðŸ’¬ [176] anonengineering (3:45:50 PM)
what do you see?
The HC-05 Bluetooth module uses blinking LED patterns to indicate its status. Rapid flashing (around 2 Hz) indicates communication mode, not connected. Two rapid flashes followed by a longer pause signify communication mode, connected. A slow, steady flash (on for 2 seconds, off for 2 seconds) indicates the module is in AT mode, ready for configuration commands.

ðŸ‘‰ I recommend checking your `Device Manager â†’ Ports` to see if the Arduino is listed there & at which port... *(you can unplug & reconnect the UNO to verify the correct port ~it should disappear when unplugged & reappear when reconnected)*

ðŸ’¬ [11] toph2814 (7:36:12 AM) [REPLY]
common occurrance when you overheat the pads during soldering... too much heat will make them delaminate from the fiberboard surface

ðŸŽ¯ [12] toph2814 (7:39:43 AM) [REPLY]
okay. so that indicates that your UNO isn't responding when the IDE attempts to communicate with it during programming...
ðŸ‘‰ make sure there's nothing connected to pins 0 & 1 of the UNO board... these pins are used for communications with the IDE & PC...
ðŸ‘‰ if there's nothing connected to those pins, try hitting the [`RESET`] button twice *(to force the UNO into "bootloader" mode)*   just before hitting the button on the IDE to upload your code ...

When evaluating safety precautions, isolated exceptions do not define best practices.

Consider this scenario: Someone asks, "Should I use a parachute when jumping from a plane at 30,000 feet?"

A flawed response would be: "Technically, there was one person who jumped without a parachute and survived for almost four minutes, so you should be fine without one."

This reasoning ignores the overwhelming evidence supporting parachute use for survival. An outlier event should not override established safety principles.

ðŸ’¬ [28] _ceratophrys_ (2:57:25 PM)
does anyone know how to setup a ramps with some nema 17 steppers? i have a 12v 10a psu and 5 DRV8825 drivers. i think i got the wiring right, i mesured where the least resistance is for the two coils inside the stepper (pins 13 and 46)
this is my code:

ðŸ’¬ [29] _ceratophrys_ (2:57:55 PM)
#define E_STEP_PIN 26
#define E_DIR_PIN 28
#define E_ENABLE_PIN 24

void setup() {
  pinMode(E_STEP_PIN, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(E_DIR_PIN, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(E_ENABLE_PIN, OUTPUT);

  digitalWrite(E_ENABLE_PIN, LOW);
}

void loop() {
  digitalWrite(E_DIR_PIN, HIGH);
  for (int i = 0; i < 200; i++) { // 200 Schritte = 1 Umdrehung bei 1.8Â°/Step
    digitalWrite(E_STEP_PIN, HIGH);
    delayMicroseconds(1000); // Step-Impulsdauer
    digitalWrite(E_STEP_PIN, LOW);
    delayMicroseconds(1000);
  }

  delay(1000); 

  digitalWrite(E_DIR_PIN, LOW);
  for (int i = 0; i < 200; i++) {
    digitalWrite(E_STEP_PIN, HIGH);
    delayMicroseconds(1000);
    digitalWrite(E_STEP_PIN, LOW);
    delayMicroseconds(1000);
  }

  delay(1000);
}

ðŸ’¬ [2] ostenjoyer3103 (1:05:56 PM)
Guys when im done with arduino for the day, can I just unplug the arduino and other stuff with all the code still on there or do i have to first upload a blank sketch then unplug it?

ðŸ’¬ [3] akumasaru (1:08:59 PM) [REPLY]
Unplugging the Arduino just gives you power loss.  The code you have on there is not lost due to power loss.  So when you turn it on, it should still have your most current project code on it, and run those functions.  Unless something happened to the device or the reset button was pushed, etc.  It won't change code until you connect it to the IDE again and upload new/older code to the device.

ðŸ’¬ [4] ostenjoyer3103 (1:10:26 PM) [REPLY]
Im not worried about it wiping the code, im just asking can i damage the board by unplugging it while running a code and powering an led?

ðŸ’¬ [5] akumasaru (1:11:15 PM)
no

ðŸ’¬ [6] ostenjoyer3103 (1:11:24 PM)
Okay thanks

ðŸ’¬ [7] toph2814 (1:13:23 PM) [REPLY]
ðŸ¤” what type of battery is it? it's possible that the battery doesn't have enough "juice"/current to power your NANO+attached components which is why its voltage is dropping *(thanks to Ohm's Law)*...

ðŸŽ¯ [8] toph2814 (1:14:28 PM) [REPLY]
yup... absolutely no worries about causing any damage... unlike PCs with operating systems, microcontrollers don't have the same pitfalls of sudden power loss which is why they're often used in appliances & other wearable electronics...

ðŸ’¬ [1] browniezoulou (1:04:07 PM)
I am having trouble using 4 slaves on my arduino mega.
I made a bus for clock, MISO, MOSI and Reset pin using the SPI pins (just learned about that)
But now I dont now what pins I am supposed to use for my Save Select
Online I find that I only have pin 53 as slave select, but if I have multiple slave, how do I use it?

For now, I connected my slaves on pins 45 to 48 and weirdly, only two of my four slaves are working correctly

ðŸŽ¯ [2] browniezoulou (1:04:33 PM)
I guess that's a hardware problem, but maybe I am coding something bad. What should I check?

ðŸ’¬ [3] browniezoulou (1:06:29 PM)
Maybe I am supposed to use pwm pins? But then again, I only have three

ðŸ’¬ [4] _wee_gee_ (1:19:27 PM) [REPLY]
> But now I dont now what pins I am supposed to use for my Save Select
If you're doing SPI, usually each individual slave has it's own separate SS pin.
You'd use any other general digital pin. Doesn't need to be PWM or anything fancy, just any pin that can be used as a digital pin.
Also, make sure you don't have multiple slave devices selected at once.

> I guess that's a hardware problem, but maybe I am coding something bad. What should I check?
Try each device individually with all others disconnected from the bus, with small snippets of example code that tests ONE slave device. If they work individually, then it's probably something with your code when running them all at the same time, likely selecting multiple devices at once.

ðŸ’¬ [5] browniezoulou (1:22:53 PM) [REPLY]
I have 3 out of 4 of my slaves working for some reason, I'll isolate the one not working to see what happen

ðŸ’¬ [4] _wee_gee_ (8:37:21 PM)
and to +1 on what others were saying, there's no kernel interrupts (since there's no kernel/OS/whatever) on an Arduino (unless you write your own or use an RTOS or similar)
There ARE interrupts though, but you need to take care when using them

ðŸ’¬ [5] _wee_gee_ (8:37:52 PM)
interrupts cannot take so long that an interrupt interrupts it's own interrupt handler, for instance

```ino
#define MA0 7
#define MA1 6
#define MB1 5
#define MB0 4

void init_motor_driver(void) {
  pinMode(MA0, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(MA1, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(MB0, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(MB1, OUTPUT);

  digitalWrite(MA0, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MA1, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MB0, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MB1, LOW);
}

void stop(void) {
  digitalWrite(MA0, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MA1, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MB0, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MB1, LOW);
}

void move_foward(void) {
  digitalWrite(MA1, HIGH);
  digitalWrite(MB1, HIGH);

  digitalWrite(MA0, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MB0, LOW);
}

void move_backwards(void) {
  digitalWrite(MA1, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MB1, LOW);

  digitalWrite(MA0, HIGH);
  digitalWrite(MB0, HIGH);
}

void rotate_left_steap(void) {
  digitalWrite(MA1, HIGH);
  digitalWrite(MB1, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MA0, LOW);
  digitalWrite(MB0, LOW);
}
```

ðŸ’¬ [25] _wee_gee_ (9:00:37 PM) [REPLY]
`digitalWrite` is about as fast as a few thousand times per second minimum (there's a little overhead when doing `digitalWrite` instead of directly manipulating the port registers) so that's probably not the issue

You're using a lot of `#define`d constants; I'd suggest using
-`enum`s for the different states (so you just declare them all at once)
-`const int`s for anything that doesn't change during program execution (sidesteps the problem of `#define`s being just fancy find-and-replace that can have weird bugs if you're not used to looking for them)


On the actual code itself, I think `distance` not being `volatile` is probably causing one of your issues.
Since `distance` is being updated by `ping`, which I assume is being called via a timer interrupt somewhere within the `NewPing` library, you need to tell the compiler "always re-load this value from it's location in memory; never assume it's not changed between the last time you read it"

I still don't see how you're observing that the loop is too slow. 
State exactly what you SEE (or observe), and what you EXPECT to see, and how they're different

ðŸ’¬ [27] _wee_gee_ (9:03:27 PM)
so you could use the typical ultrasonic sensor code but with a strict timeout that's not a full second (which is the default for `pulseIn`)

ðŸ’¬ [37] _wee_gee_ (9:10:17 PM)
> I don't know how to tell the compiler distance is volatile
it's standard C++, a google search should point you in the right direction too, but it's just `volatile int distance = -1;`; just a different kind of variable
>  i expected #define to be more performant than const int
the compiler's smarter than that. `const int` also lets the compiler check that you didn't make a mistake like using a `const unsigned int` where you wanted a `const int`
`const int` also makes it so you don't get weird order-of-operations thing happen like this:
```ino
#define MY_CONSTANT 6+7
void setup(){
  Serial.println(MY_CONSTANT*3);
}
```
this doesn't necessaily print `(6+7)*3`, it might print `6+(7*3)`
tl;dr the compiler is smart enough to strip out things like that and replace them with their equivalents.

If you don't believe me, [Compiler Explorer](https://godbolt.org/) has a mode to compile code for Arduino boards etc. so you can see the assembly has no difference between `const`s and `#define`s

ðŸ’¬ [38] otocotara (9:11:13 PM)

ðŸ’¬ [40] _wee_gee_ (9:13:45 PM)
> and i never delt with interupts, so i dont know how to make it work
I thought you were asking earlier about kernel interrupts?
The general idea is you can have events call functions, "interrupting" the main loop. Very useful especially for things like this which need to respond quickly (or to unpredictable inputs)

ðŸ’¬ [79] _wee_gee_ (9:35:59 PM)
ISRs are "function that gets immediately called when an interrupt happens, pausing whatever else was happening at the time"; it's an Interrupt Service Routine

If however, an interrupt interrupts an ISR, then there's high chance for issues to arise, especially if you "pause" the ISR to... go do the ISR
eventually something will do unpredictable things

ðŸ’¬ [80] _wee_gee_ (9:36:35 PM)
-> you want to finish the ISR asap so that 
-the code being interrupted doesn't notice
-another interrupt won't happen while the ISR is still not done

ðŸ’¬ [81] _wee_gee_ (9:37:34 PM)
whenever you use an Arduino (at least an Uno) with the Arduino IDE, you have interrupts going "in the background" all the time; the interrupt that updates `millis` for you. 
It's so fast though that it barely registers unless you're doing microsecond-accurate work

ðŸ’¬ [82] _wee_gee_ (9:38:22 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸ’¬ [1] toph2814 (11:27:54 PM)
ðŸ¤” perhaps consider using a *Pro Micro*  or *RP2040*  based board *(like the `Arduino NANO RP2040 Connect`)*  which are both popular choices for custom HID keyboards & controllers...

ðŸ’¬ [2] toph2814 (11:30:12 PM)
*( ðŸ¤· you'll need to do a bit of digging regarding that... but I haven't heard as many issues with the Pro Micro as compared to the Leonardo for HID applications)*

ðŸ’¬ [3] toph2814 (11:32:55 PM)
it's a great board but it's "too new" for my taste... vs other older boards which are more widely supported

ðŸ’¬ [4] toph2814 (11:33:35 PM)
you could buy some boards with presoldered headers

ðŸ’¬ [5] toph2814 (11:37:19 PM)
yup... they did though I haven't seen them on sale lately...

ðŸ’¬ [6] toph2814 (11:40:06 PM)
might be worth investing in a good soldering iron & learning a very useful skill for tinkering

ðŸ’¬ [7] toph2814 (11:42:43 PM) [REPLY]
roughly $30ish for a *Pinecil V2 (or V1)*  but that doesn't come with a power supply *(you'll need around 65W or better)*

ðŸ’¬ [8] toph2814 (11:43:31 PM)
*(they can be that high for branded smart irons like `Hakko` or `Weller`)*

ðŸ’¬ [9] toph2814 (11:44:03 PM)
*the key is that they need proper internal PID temperature control*

ðŸ’¬ [10] toph2814 (11:46:50 PM)
also understand that there is a massive size difference between an `Arduino R4 Minima` vs other boards *(like the Pro Micro, NANO, & the stamp-sized boards that are gaining popularity)* ... which makes the smaller form factor of other boards more attractive for portable HID controllers

ðŸ’¬ [11] toph2814 (11:47:57 PM)
again, depends on what you're actually building ðŸ˜‰

ðŸ’¬ [12] toph2814 (11:49:05 PM)
one big advantage of larger boards are their available GPIO pins ðŸ¤“ but that can be mitigated by shift registers & IÂ²C port expanders

ðŸ’¬ [13] toph2814 (11:50:33 PM)
or course...

ðŸŽ¯ [14] toph2814 (11:54:01 PM)
ðŸ‘‰ HID is a standard for computer peripherals...
ðŸ‘‰ Arduino is an ecosystem *(board + IDE + libraries)*  that allow you to create microcontroller projects relatively easily...

ðŸŽ¯ [15] toph2814 (11:55:19 PM)
ðŸ¤“ so any microcontroller board that is "HID compliant" can be made into an HID peripheral by using the appropriate libraries that allow it to communicate with the computer it's attached to via USB...

ðŸŽ¯ [16] toph2814 (11:55:32 PM)
*(aka the "firmware")*

ðŸ’¬ [17] toph2814 (11:57:29 PM)
then unless you're simply copying an existing HID controller project, then building a car SIM controller from scratch is fairly intermediate to advanced

ðŸŽ¯ [18] toph2814 (11:57:39 PM)
*(electronics + programming -wise)*

ðŸ’¬ [19] toph2814 (12:00:51 AM)
haven't really looked into `Hoodloader2` myself but as it will be messing/reprogramming the onboard chip to force an UNO into HID compliance, I definitely wouldn't recommend it... *(specially since there are plenty of comparatively low cost out of the box HID compliant boards out there)*

ðŸ’¬ [20] toph2814 (12:02:24 AM)
ðŸ‘‰ as a beginner, I'd recommend following the guide to the letter *(with the board they specifically used)* ... then you'll hope it's a good guide *(at least one who's not faking their results; if it's a guide that tells you to use `Hoodloader2` then I'd look for a different one)*

ðŸ’¬ [21] toph2814 (12:04:54 AM)
again. I don't condone this practice *(relying on generative AI or forcing an UNO to a task it wasn't meant for)*  so if that's the path you wish to go with... all I can say is good luck with your project...

ðŸ’¬ [22] toph2814 (12:07:06 AM)

ðŸ’¬ [19] m_a_v_e_r_i_c_k (3:36:34 PM) [REPLY]
Sounds like it just reset it and did not run the bootloader. Arduino says to hold down the reset button on a Leonardo, click the upload button and do not realease it until you see "Uploading...". Maybe Arduino used different bootloaders for these two and your boards have the wrong one. Or the bootloader has been corrupted, or the firmware on the board is causing a problem. Not sure.

vcc > 3.3v
gnd > gnd
scl > a5
sda > a4

code:
```c++
#include <Wire.h>
#include <Adafruit_Sensor.h>
#include <Adafruit_ADXL345_U.h>

Adafruit_ADXL345_Unified accel = Adafruit_ADXL345_Unified();

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(9600);
}

void loop() {
  sensors_event_t event;
  accel.getEvent(&event);

  Serial.print("X: "); Serial.print(event.acceleration.x); Serial.print(" ");
  Serial.print("Y: "); Serial.print(event.acceleration.y); Serial.print(" ");
  Serial.print("Z: "); Serial.print(event.acceleration.z); Serial.print(" "); Serial.println("m/s^2");

  delay(100);
}
```

ðŸ’¬ [17] tuulikauri (10:15:37 PM) [REPLY]
Do you know how to add print statements to help debug? You can add them to each of the next functions. You are seeing the `Accelerometer test` statement, but the rest of the setup should print other stuff too.
```ino

  Serial.println("Accelerometer Test"); Serial.println("");
  
  /* Initialise the sensor */
  if(!accel.begin())
  {
    /* There was a problem detecting the ADXL345 ... check your connections */
    Serial.println("Ooops, no ADXL345 detected ... Check your wiring!");
    while(1);
  }

  /* Set the range to whatever is appropriate for your project */
  accel.setRange(ADXL345_RANGE_16_G);
  // accel.setRange(ADXL345_RANGE_8_G);
  // accel.setRange(ADXL345_RANGE_4_G);
  // accel.setRange(ADXL345_RANGE_2_G);
  
  /* Display some basic information on this sensor */
  displaySensorDetails();
  
  /* Display additional settings (outside the scope of sensor_t) */
  displayDataRate();
  displayRange();
  Serial.println("");```

ðŸ’¬ [18] tuulikauri (10:16:23 PM)
Figuring out if it finishes setup() or where it stops, if it stops before finishing, will give you a clue on what might be going wrong.

ðŸ’¬ [2] toph2814 (2:59:44 AM) [REPLY]
you'll definitely need a "`boost converter`" in addition to the TP4056 lithium battery charging module to bring the voltage to a constant 5V *(which is what the RPi wants)* ...
â˜ï¸ and yes, with the TP4056 you can charge while using....
ðŸ¤“ of course, you'd be better off with a powerbank module *(since it already has both the charging + cell balancing circuit & voltage regulation to provide a 5V output)* ... 
 ... âš ï¸ however you need to make sure that the powerbank module can supply __at least 2A__ of power out of its USB-A port *(this should be good as long as you don't plug too many power hungry devices on your RPi's USB ports)* ... naturally 3A would be best so there's little to no chance of undervoltage warnings âš¡...

ðŸ’¬ [3] toph2814 (3:00:38 AM)
ðŸ¤“ ðŸ‘‰ note that if you're going the "`5v boost converter`" route, make sure it can supply 3A as well ðŸ˜‰

ðŸ’¬ [132] toph2814 (5:28:33 AM) [REPLY]
a switch? 
ðŸ¤” depends on what you're turning "off" ... 
ðŸ‘‰ since you'll be running an operating system on your RPi3, you should hook up a momentary pushbutton to one of its GPIO pin that will trigger your shutdown routine *(otherwise you run the risk of currupting your Linux filesystem)*...
ðŸ‘‰ if you also want to be able to turn off power to the RPi3 to preserve battery power, you can place it between the TP4056 & the boost converter *(you can turn off just the `+` with a SPST toggle switch or both `+` & `-` with a SPDT one)*...
âš ï¸ just don't place it after the boost converter .... because those circuits typically don't like it when you power it up with no load *(hence the dummy load resistor I suggested earlier)* ...

all GPIO pins can be used... but know that pins 0 & 1 are tied to RX & TX for UART *(the USB serial connection to your PC & Arduino's IDE)*  respectively @Dylan ....

ðŸ’¬ [2] toph2814 (9:40:47 AM)
â˜ï¸ so if you do use those pins, you can't use the Serial Monitor or use `Serial` for input & output...

ðŸ’¬ [3] toph2814 (9:41:29 AM)
*(using them also affects programming/uploading a new sketch/code to the board)*

ðŸ’¬ [4] toph2814 (9:42:46 AM)
â˜ï¸ definitely reduces the hassle of having to disconnect anything on pins 0 & 1 every time you want to update the UNO's code...

ðŸ’¬ [44] toph2814 (10:38:18 AM) [REPLY]
no... 
ðŸ¤“ take note that voltage is not a suggestion... for most components, they are made to work at that specific voltage *(think of a 110V appliance used in a country with 220V mains)* ...
ðŸ‘‰ LEDs typically can be over driven *(using a higher voltage on them than they're rated for)*  and although it will often allow the LEDs to get brighter, their life will be shortened since they will burn out sooner than later.

ðŸ’¬ [45] toph2814 (10:40:24 AM)
ðŸ¤“ to determine how much current your project requires, you look at each component's datasheet *(microcontroller, LEDs, sensors, motors, etc)*  to see what current they will need... add all of them together... to get the total current requirement...

ðŸ’¬ [46] toph2814 (10:42:09 AM)
ðŸ‘‰ so just to clarify, the key is the amount of current each LED will pull... for standard 5mm LEDs, that's usually upto 20mA... for super bright "cob"-type LEDs, they typically range for 1W to 5W *(so 200mA to as high as 1A)* ... so 15 of them would mean you'd multiply the current requirements...
â˜ï¸ this will then dictate the amount of power your power supply needs to provide at 5V... so for 15x 1W LEDs == 3A *(or a 15W power supply with 5V output)*  minimum *(a 30W power supply will work fine, just means it has a big enough "reservoir" to handle more current than is needed)*...
ðŸ˜„ hope this clears things up...

ðŸ’¬ [24] .pseud0 (5:49:55 PM)
```cpp

#include <Wire.h>
 
 
void setup()
{
  Wire.begin();
 
  Serial.begin(9600);
  while (!Serial);             // Leonardo: wait for serial monitor
  Serial.println("\nI2C Scanner");
}
 
 
void loop()
{
  byte error, address;
  int nDevices;
 
  Serial.println("Scanning...");
 
  nDevices = 0;
  for(address = 1; address < 127; address++ )
  {
    // The i2c_scanner uses the return value of
    // the Write.endTransmisstion to see if
    // a device did acknowledge to the address.
    Wire.beginTransmission(address);
    error = Wire.endTransmission();
 
    if (error == 0)
    {
      Serial.print("I2C device found at address 0x");
      if (address<16)
        Serial.print("0");
      Serial.print(address,HEX);
      Serial.println("  !");
 
      nDevices++;
    }
    else if (error==4)
    {
      Serial.print("Unknown error at address 0x");
      if (address<16)
        Serial.print("0");
      Serial.println(address,HEX);
    }    
  }
  if (nDevices == 0)
    Serial.println("No I2C devices found\n");
  else
    Serial.println("done\n");
 
  delay(5000);           // wait 5 seconds for next scan
}
```
is the sketch code

ðŸ’¬ [89] .pseud0 (6:17:47 PM) [REPLY]
in regards to these three pads you can use your multimeter to measure:
* red lead in the middle pad, black lead on ground of the arduino (e.g. USB connector shield): gives you the supply voltage of the PCA9.. chip. should be +5V. If it's 0 then the PCA chip has no power. then the middle pad should be connected to the lower 5V pad with a blob of solder.
* red lead on the bottom pad, black lead on ground of the arduino (e.g. USB connector shield): gives you the 5V voltage on the board. should be +5V.

ðŸ’¬ [90] .pseud0 (6:18:15 PM)
if it has power, then check continuity between the SCL <--> SCL and SDA <--> SDA connection. (arduino <-> servo board).

ðŸŽ¯ [91] .pseud0 (6:18:42 PM)
if the servo board has power and the i2c lines are connected properly, it **should** be showing up on the I2c scanner..
Should also be a direct connection between the Arduino's GND pin and the servo shield's GND pin

ðŸ’¬ [92] .pseud0 (6:19:19 PM)
reference pictures of that board have a visible trace between the middle pad and the lower 5V pad

ðŸ’¬ [1] noobmaster699551 (5:58:41 PM)
Hay guys how do i power my esp32 with rechargeable batteries does anyone know what hardware i need? Cus i csnt find a YouTube vid thats reliable in my opinion so does anyone know?

ðŸ’¬ [2] klevi_ (7:04:15 PM) [REPLY]
Well, depending on your particular ESP32, the onboard regulator might be able to convert the voltage of a single 18650 lithium ion cell to the desired 3.3V. For that the droupout voltage needs to be very low.

What regulator does your ESP32 use? Google the datasheet of it and find the dropout voltage to depermine whether it can be used so easily.

If not, there are still many other solutions.

ðŸ’¬ [3] klevi_ (7:07:38 PM)
Or just use a powerbank

ðŸŽ¯ [4] klevi_ (7:08:17 PM)
There are quite small powerbanks. They are handy as you don't risk damaging your electronics

ðŸ’¬ [1] ritizzzz (7:40:12 AM)
Hello, I have made a custom sensing pcb using a ina239 IC. I have been trying to establish comms with it for the past day and a half and I am not getting anywhere. The board is based on the evaluation by TI. Here is the code: 

```
#include <SPI.h>

#define CS_PIN 10

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(9600);

  // Start SPI at the slowest clock (125 kHz), Mode 1
  SPI.begin();
  SPI.beginTransaction(SPISettings(125000, MSBFIRST, SPI_MODE1));

  pinMode(CS_PIN, OUTPUT);
  digitalWrite(CS_PIN, HIGH); // deselect chip
  delay(100);
}

void loop() {
  uint16_t result = readRegister(0x3F);  
  Serial.print("Manufacturer ID: 0x");
  Serial.println(result, HEX);
  delay(100);
}

uint16_t readRegister(uint8_t reg) {
  uint16_t value = 0;
  uint8_t addr = (reg << 2) | 0x01; // shift left 2, add 1 for READ

  digitalWrite(CS_PIN, LOW);
  SPI.transfer(addr);               // send register address
  value = (SPI.transfer(0x00) << 8); // high byte
  value |= SPI.transfer(0x00);       // low byte
  digitalWrite(CS_PIN, HIGH);

  return value;
}
```

Am I missing something really stupid?

ðŸ’¬ [2] .pseud0 (7:48:39 AM) [REPLY]
So what does it read via SPI? what's your microcontroller? that's not shown in the schematic.

ðŸ’¬ [3] ritizzzz (7:50:09 AM)
I am using an arduino uno. It is not reading anything at the moment. Just 0x00. It should be reading 0x5449

ðŸ’¬ [4] ritizzzz (7:50:42 AM)
here is the pcb:

ðŸ’¬ [5] .pseud0 (7:51:17 AM)

those are kelvin connections to the chpi, teh traces through the shunt are the big copper pads

ðŸ’¬ [8] ritizzzz (7:53:19 AM) [REPLY]
pin 10 -> CS
pin 11 -> MOSI
pin 12 -> MISO 
pin 13 -> SCLK
5v -> 5v
GND -> GND

ðŸ’¬ [13] .pseud0 (7:57:34 AM) [REPLY]
compared to the reference code (<https://github.com/RobTillaart/INA239/blob/a55a97254b858ff3f604ea47f265b18824b35a07/INA239.cpp#L531-L560>) you're only missing the wrapping of beginTransaction and endTransaction around every SPI transaction

ðŸ’¬ [34] .pseud0 (8:07:00 AM)
I would write with begin/end transaction as 
```cpp
#include <SPI.h>

#define CS_PIN 10

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(9600);

  // Start SPI at the slowest clock (125 kHz), Mode 1
  SPI.begin();

  pinMode(CS_PIN, OUTPUT);
  digitalWrite(CS_PIN, HIGH); // deselect chip
  delay(100);
}

void loop() {
  uint16_t result = readRegister(0x3F);  
  Serial.print("Manufacturer ID: 0x");
  Serial.println(result, HEX);
  delay(100);
}

uint16_t readRegister(uint8_t reg) {
  uint16_t value = 0;
  uint8_t addr = (reg << 2) | 0x01; // shift left 2, add 1 for READ

  digitalWrite(CS_PIN, LOW);
  SPI.beginTransaction(SPISettings(125000, MSBFIRST, SPI_MODE1));
  SPI.transfer(addr);               // send register address
  value = (SPI.transfer(0x00) << 8u); // high byte
  value |= SPI.transfer(0x00);       // low byte
  SPI.endTransaction();
  digitalWrite(CS_PIN, HIGH);

  return value;
}
```

ðŸ’¬ [35] .pseud0 (8:07:11 AM)

yep nothign with library code as well 

```
//
//    FILE: INA239_demo.ino
//  AUTHOR: Rob Tillaart
// PURPOSE: demo core functions
//     URL: https://github.com/RobTillaart/INA239


#include "INA239.h"

//  select, dataIn, dataOut, clock == SOFTWARE SPI
//  INA239 INA(5, 6, 7, 8);

//  select, &SPI === HW SPI
INA239 INA(10, &SPI);


void setup()
{
  Serial.begin(115200);
  Serial.println(__FILE__);
  Serial.println();
  Serial.print("INA239_LIB_VERSION: ");
  Serial.println(INA239_LIB_VERSION);
  Serial.println();

  SPI.begin();

  if (!INA.begin() )
  {
    Serial.println("Could not connect. Fix and Reboot");
    while(1);
  }

  INA.setMaxCurrentShunt(10, 0.015);

}


void loop()
{
  Serial.println("\nVBUS\tVSHUNT\tCURRENT\tPOWER\tTEMP");
  for (int i = 0; i < 20; i++)
  {
    Serial.print(INA.getBusVoltage(), 3);
    Serial.print("\t");
    Serial.print(INA.getShuntMilliVolt(), 3);
    Serial.print("\t");
    Serial.print(INA.getMilliAmpere(), 3);
    Serial.print("\t");
    Serial.print(INA.getMilliWatt(), 3);
    Serial.print("\t");
    Serial.print(INA.getTemperature(), 3);
    Serial.println();
    delay(1000);
  }
}


//  -- END OF FILE --
```

ðŸ’¬ [17] .pseud0 (8:22:40 AM)
the DFRobot core uses this config
```
firebeetle8266.name=DFRobot FireBeetle-ESP8266

firebeetle8266.upload.tool=esptool
firebeetle8266.upload.speed=460800
firebeetle8266.upload.resetmethod=nodemcu
firebeetle8266.upload.maximum_size=1044464
firebeetle8266.upload.maximum_data_size=81920
firebeetle8266.upload.wait_for_upload_port=true
firebeetle8266.serial.disableDTR=false
firebeetle8266.serial.disableRTS=false

firebeetle8266.build.mcu=esp8266
firebeetle8266.build.f_cpu=80000000L
firebeetle8266.build.board=FireBeetle-ESP8266
firebeetle8266.build.core=esp8266
firebeetle8266.build.variant=firebeetle8266
firebeetle8266.build.flash_mode=dio
firebeetle8266.build.flash_size=4M
firebeetle8266.build.flash_freq=40
firebeetle8266.build.debug_port=
firebeetle8266.build.debug_level=

firebeetle8266.menu.CpuFrequency.80=80 MHz
firebeetle8266.menu.CpuFrequency.80.build.f_cpu=80000000L
firebeetle8266.menu.CpuFrequency.160=160 MHz
firebeetle8266.menu.CpuFrequency.160.build.f_cpu=160000000L

firebeetle8266.menu.UploadSpeed.921600=921600


firebeetle8266.menu.FlashSize.4M3M=4M (3M SPIFFS)
firebeetle8266.menu.FlashSize.4M1M=4M (1M SPIFFS)
```

ðŸ’¬ [27] .pseud0 (8:30:16 AM)
Test the LED blink sketch first 
```cpp
// the setup function runs once when you press reset or power the board
void setup() {
  // initialize digital pin LED_BUILTIN as an output.
  pinMode(LED_BUILTIN, OUTPUT);
}

// the loop function runs over and over again forever
void loop() {
  digitalWrite(LED_BUILTIN, HIGH);  // turn the LED on (HIGH is the voltage level)
  delay(1000);                      // wait for a second
  digitalWrite(LED_BUILTIN, LOW);   // turn the LED off by making the voltage LOW
  delay(1000);                      // wait for a second
}
```
It should still blink IO2.

```c
#include <Adafruit_GFX.h>    
#include <Adafruit_ST7735.h> 
#include <SPI.h>


#define TFT_CS    10
#define TFT_RST   8  
#define TFT_DC    9 

#define TFT_SCLK 13   
#define TFT_MOSI 11   

//Adafruit_ST7735 tft = Adafruit_ST7735(TFT_CS,  TFT_DC, TFT_RST);
Adafruit_ST7735 tft = Adafruit_ST7735(TFT_CS, TFT_DC, TFT_MOSI, TFT_SCLK, TFT_RST);

void setup(void) {
  Serial.begin(9600);

  tft.initR(INITR_BLACKTAB);  
  tft.fillScreen(ST7735_BLACK); 
 
  tft.setTextColor(ST7735_WHITE);
  tft.setTextSize(0);
  tft.setCursor(30,80);
  tft.println("Hello World!");  
}

void loop() {
}
```

This code has the fixes that I said earlier, and trying the pot power with an multimeter is a good idea ( @Fireguy9143 ). Make sure the wiring is correct ðŸ‘ 


#include <Adafruit_GFX.h>
#include <Adafruit_ST7735.h>
#include <SPI.h>

#define TFT_CS   10
#define TFT_DC    9
#define TFT_RST   8

Adafruit_ST7735 tft = Adafruit_ST7735(TFT_CS, TFT_DC, TFT_RST);

void setup() {
  tft.initR(INITR_BLACKTAB);   // try INITR_REDTAB if colors/distortion appear
  tft.fillScreen(ST77XX_GREEN);

  tft.setTextColor(ST77XX_WHITE);
  tft.setTextSize(1);
  tft.setCursor(30, 80);
  tft.println("Hello World!");
}

void loop() {
}

ðŸŽ¯ [28] anonengineering (11:17:42 AM)
to test your hardware you don't even need an Uno
each resistor has one end on the 7 seg, the other end goes to a pin
if COM goes to ground you can connect a jumper from 5v to the end on each resistor that goes to a pin (disconnect from Uno first)
then you'll know which resistor is which segment (A - G and the decimal point)

THREAD #205

Participants: toph2814, elaiire
Messages: 4 | Duration: 55 minutes
Time: 9/23/2025, 12:25:45 AM
======================================================================

ðŸ’¬ [1] toph2814 (12:25:45 AM)
fyi/clarification @elaiire, these are just some of the popular `microcontrollers` and the (development boards) you will typically find them in: 
ðŸ‘‰ `STM32`, `ESP8266`(NodeMCU)  | `ESP32` (many variants on many DevKits), `RP2040`(RPi Pico) | `RP2350`(RPi Pico 2), `ATmega328P`(UNO, NANO)  | `ATmega2560`(MEGA) ... 
â˜ï¸ the microcontroller is effectively the processor IC that serves as the brains of the microcontroller development board which are custom PCBs that contain all the necessary support electronics to make the microcontroller function & allow you to make connections to the IC to send & receive signals to do different tasks...

ðŸ’¬ [2] toph2814 (12:28:25 AM)
ðŸ¤“ meanwhile "`Arduino`" is the company & the workflow infrastructure they've built *(Arduino IDE & core libraries)*  that allows us to program these microcontrollers relatively easier than before...

ðŸŽ¯ [3] toph2814 (12:32:21 AM)
*(oh, they've also provided the schematics for their microcontroller boards so everyone can effectively build their own... which is why we have so many knockoffs & clones, even branded clones out there for us to choose from)*

ðŸ’¬ [4] elaiire (1:20:48 AM) [REPLY]
Thank youu!

THREAD #210

Time: 9/28/2025, 8:42:06 AM
======================================================================

ðŸ’¬ [1] tahagamer11 (8:42:06 AM)
@Maverick hi Can i know what is it i accedently burn it by plug 7votle in 3.3v output

ðŸŽ¯ [2] penpengu (8:45:12 AM) [REPLY]
That chip is the USB<->UART converter.
Without it, communication with the Serial Monitor on the PC, and uploading new programs to the arduino won't work.

ðŸ’¬ [3] tahagamer11 (8:45:42 AM)
ah ok thanks

ðŸŽ¯ [4] penpengu (8:46:30 AM)
But the arduino itself can still work if only that chip is broken.
You will need a second arduino or a USB-serial converter to upload new programs to the half-broken arduino or use the serial monitor with the half-broken arduino.

ðŸ’¬ [5] penpengu (8:47:00 AM)
You could use that half-broken arduino in a permanent installation

ðŸ’¬ [6] tahagamer11 (8:47:40 AM)
thanks

ðŸ’¬ [7] tahagamer11 (8:48:05 AM) [REPLY]
thanks for your time

ðŸ’¬ [8] penpengu (8:48:28 AM)
no problem ðŸ˜„ Have fun (with hopefully a second non-broken arduino ðŸ˜„ )

ðŸ’¬ [9] .pseud0 (1:59:13 PM) [REPLY]
Okay, now we can talk. Upload this I2C scanner sketch to your Uno 
```cpp
#include <Wire.h>
 
void setup() {
  Wire.begin();
 
  Serial.begin(9600);
  while (!Serial);             // Leonardo: wait for serial monitor
  Serial.println("\nI2C Scanner");
}
 
void loop() {
  byte error, address;
  int nDevices;
 
  Serial.println("Scanning...");
 
  nDevices = 0;
  for(address = 1; address < 127; address++ )
  {
    // The i2c_scanner uses the return value of
    // the Write.endTransmisstion to see if
    // a device did acknowledge to the address.
    Wire.beginTransmission(address);
    error = Wire.endTransmission();
 
    if (error == 0)
    {
      Serial.print("I2C device found at address 0x");
      if (address<16)
        Serial.print("0");
      Serial.print(address,HEX);
      Serial.println("  !");
 
      nDevices++;
    }
    else if (error==4)
    {
      Serial.print("Unknown error at address 0x");
      if (address<16)
        Serial.print("0");
      Serial.println(address,HEX);
    }    
  }
  if (nDevices == 0)
    Serial.println("No I2C devices found\n");
  else
    Serial.println("done\n");
 
  delay(5000);           // wait 5 seconds for next scan
}
```

ðŸ’¬ [47] .pseud0 (2:18:20 PM)
```cpp
#include <Arduino.h>
#include <U8g2lib.h>

// U8G2_SH1106_128X64_NONAME_F_HW_I2C  -> Full framebuffer mode (we do NOT want this)
// U8G2_SH1106_128X64_NONAME_1_HW_I2C  -> Page buffer mode (uses ~128 bytes, updates pagewise)

// Create display object: HW I2C, no reset pin
U8G2_SH1106_128X64_NONAME_1_HW_I2C u8g2(U8G2_R0, /* reset=*/ U8X8_PIN_NONE);

void setup() {
  u8g2.begin();
  u8g2.setFont(u8g2_font_ncenB08_tr); // small readable font
}

void loop() {
  // Pagewise drawing: begin(), draw(), nextPage()
  u8g2.firstPage();
  do {
    // simple test pattern
    u8g2.drawStr(0, 10, "U8g2 SH1106 Test");
    u8g2.drawHLine(0, 12, 128);
    u8g2.setCursor(0, 28);
    u8g2.print("Pagewise mode");
    u8g2.setCursor(0, 44);
    u8g2.print("No framebuffer");
    u8g2.setCursor(0, 60);
    u8g2.print(millis() / 1000);
  } while (u8g2.nextPage());

  delay(1000);
}
```

ðŸ’¬ [48] .pseud0 (2:18:28 PM)
Does the display still show stuff? Powercycle if needed.

ðŸ’¬ [49] .pseud0 (2:20:07 PM)
Check the dynamic memory consumption. Should be much lower.

ðŸ’¬ [50] .pseud0 (2:21:31 PM)
Exactly, only 34% instead of 76%

ðŸ’¬ [51] .pseud0 (2:21:56 PM)
The Adafruit one uses a full framebuffer for the display (128 x 64 / 8 bytes = 1024 bytes)

ðŸ’¬ [52] .pseud0 (2:22:08 PM)
But the u8g2 can do only a single page buffer of 128 byte

ðŸ’¬ [53] .pseud0 (2:22:24 PM)
so your sketch can have more logic and use more RAM if you use u8g2.

ðŸ’¬ [54] .pseud0 (2:23:00 PM)
Usually the SH1106 display has a "RESET" pin which cleanly resets the display for each run

ðŸ’¬ [55] .pseud0 (2:23:13 PM)
your display doesn't have that, so if it ever gets stuck in a weird state, you have to powercycle it.

ðŸ’¬ [52] mysteriouscosmic (12:43:47 PM)

ðŸ’¬ [53] .pseud0 (12:47:53 PM)
```cpp
#include <Arduino.h>
#include <TFT_eSPI.h>
#include <SPI.h>

TFT_eSPI tft = TFT_eSPI();

void setup() {
  tft.init();
  Serial.begin(115200);
}

void diag_show(void);

void loop() {
  diag_show();
  delay(2000);
}

void diag_reg(const __FlashStringHelper *name, uint8_t reg, uint8_t n)
{
    uint8_t x = reg;
    Serial.print(name);
    Serial.print(" (0x");
    Serial.print(x < 0x10 ? "0" : "");
    Serial.print(x, HEX);
    Serial.print("):");
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        uint8_t x = tft.readcommand8(reg, i);
        Serial.print(x < 0x10 ? " 0" : " ");
        Serial.print(x, HEX);
    }
    Serial.println("");
}

void diag_show(void)
{
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDDID"), 0x04, 5);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDIMGFMT"), 0x0A, 1);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDMADCTL"), 0x0B, 1);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDPIXFMT"), 0x0C, 1);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDSELFDIAG"), 0x0F, 1);

ðŸ’¬ [62] .pseud0 (12:56:15 PM)
```cpp
#include <Arduino.h>
#include <TFT_eSPI.h>
#include <SPI.h>

TFT_eSPI tft = TFT_eSPI();

void setup() {
  Serial.begin(115200);
  delay(2000);
  Serial.println("Firwmare start."); Serial.flush();
  tft.init();
  Serial.println("TFT initted."); Serial.flush();
}

void diag_show(void);

void loop() {
  Serial.println("Reading diagnostics."); Serial.flush();
  diag_show();
  delay(2000);
}

void diag_reg(const __FlashStringHelper *name, uint8_t reg, uint8_t n)
{
    uint8_t x = reg;
    Serial.print(name);
    Serial.print(" (0x");
    Serial.print(x < 0x10 ? "0" : "");
    Serial.print(x, HEX);
    Serial.print("):");
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        uint8_t x = tft.readcommand8(reg, i);
        Serial.print(x < 0x10 ? " 0" : " ");
        Serial.print(x, HEX);
    }
    Serial.println("");
}

void diag_show(void)
{
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDDID"), 0x04, 5);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDIMGFMT"), 0x0A, 1);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDMADCTL"), 0x0B, 1);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDPIXFMT"), 0x0C, 1);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDSELFDIAG"), 0x0F, 1);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_DFUNCTR"), 0xb6, 5);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_PWCTR1"), 0xC0, 3);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_VMCTR1"), 0xC5, 3);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_VMCTR2"), 0xC7, 2);
    diag_reg(F("NVM Status   "), 0xD2, 3);
    diag_reg(F("ID4          "), 0xD3, 4);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDID1"), 0xDA, 2);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDID2"), 0xDB, 2);
    diag_reg(F("ILI9488_RDID3"), 0xDC, 2);
    diag_reg(F("GAMMAP       "), 0xE0, 16);
    diag_reg(F("GAMMAN       "), 0xE1, 16);
    diag_reg(F("INTERFACE    "), 0xf6, 4);
}
```

ðŸ’¬ [112] demonking4912 (8:33:33 PM) [REPLY]

ðŸ’¬ [113] diamonddback (8:45:08 PM)
That was indeed my intention. And for your information, I understand how frustrating it can be to create the diagram at this stage. We've all been there. However, this experience should serve as a reminder to document your progress as you go.

For any project, regardless of its size, even if you're just sketching on a piece of paper with crayons and taking a picture to save in the project folder, keeping a running document is crucial. 

If you don't want to use EDA software, common tools include MS Paint, GIMP, Inkscape, Draw.io, or any PowerPoint/slide software.

Here's a suggested workflow: when starting a project, before pulling out a breadboard, create a quick draft using PowerPoint (for example). Insert an image of a breadboard, add an image of the ESP, and wire it up as you envision. Follow this draft as you work. If you discover, for instance, that pin 16 can't be used because it's input-only, first update your diagram by removing the "wire" and reconnecting it to a new pin, such as pin 34. Then replicate the changes on the actual breadboard.

By working this way, you'll always have an up-to-date wiring diagram that evolves with your project. When dealing with complex sections like motor drivers and other components, break each part into its own slide to avoid clutter. For instance, the right side of the motor driver could be on slide 12, while the left side continues on slide 13, and so on.

I also use software to maintain not only wiring diagrams but also all code revisions, ensuring everything stays organized and accessible.

If you do this any time you need help you have everything at hand, what wiring was working yesterday? here you go.. what wiring you have NOW? here you go. is it up to date? as of 5 minutes ago, yes.

ðŸ’¬ [48] thekoy0 (7:42:47 PM)
new and improved(?) code:
```
// Include the AccelStepper Library
#include <AccelStepper.h>

// Define pin connections
const int dirPin = 6;
const int stepPin = 5;
const int sleepPin = 7;
const int resetPin = 8;

// Define motor interface type
#define motorInterfaceType 1

// Creates an instance
AccelStepper myStepper(motorInterfaceType, stepPin, dirPin);

void setup() {
  // set the maximum speed, acceleration factor,
  // initial speed and the target position
  pinMode(dirPin, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(stepPin, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(resetPin, OUTPUT);
  pinMode(sleepPin, OUTPUT);
  digitalWrite(resetPin, HIGH);
  digitalWrite(sleepPin, HIGH);
  myStepper.setMaxSpeed(1000);
  myStepper.setAcceleration(50);
  myStepper.setSpeed(200);
  myStepper.moveTo(1000);
}

void loop() {
  // Change direction once the motor reaches target position
  if (myStepper.distanceToGo() == 0)
    delay(2000);
    myStepper.moveTo(-myStepper.currentPosition());

  // Move the motor one step
  myStepper.run();
}
```
